Queen"</div><div class="hlist hlist-separated" style="font-size: smaller"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Navy_Band_-_God_Save_the_Queen.ogg" title="File:United States Navy Band - God Save the Queen.ogg">file</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Media help">help</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:right"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_anthem" title="Template:Infobox anthem"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_anthem" title="Template talk:Infobox anthem"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_anthem&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>"<b>God Save the Queen</b>" (alternatively "<b>God Save the King</b>", depending on the gender of the reigning monarch) is the <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national</a> or <a href="/wiki/Honors_music" title="Honors music">royal anthem</a> in a number of <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a>, their territories, and the British <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-royal.gov.uk_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal.gov.uk-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The author of the tune is unknown, and it may originate in <a href="/wiki/Plainsong" title="Plainsong">plainchant</a>; but an attribution to the composer <a href="/wiki/John_Bull_(composer)" title="John Bull (composer)">John Bull</a> is sometimes made.\n</p><p>"God Save the Queen" is the national anthem of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and one of two national anthems used by <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> since 1977, as well as for several of the UK\'s territories that have their own additional local anthem. It is also the royal anthem \xe2\x80\x93 played specifically in the presence of the monarch \xe2\x80\x93 of all the aforementioned countries, as well as <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (since 1984), <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (since 1980),<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a>. In countries not previously part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, the tune of "God Save the Queen" has provided the basis for various patriotic songs, though still generally connected with royal ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The melody continues to be used for the national anthem of <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Oben_am_jungen_Rhein" title="Oben am jungen Rhein">Oben am jungen Rhein</a>", and the royal anthem of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Kongesangen" title="Kongesangen">Kongesangen</a>". In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the melody is used for the patriotic song "<a href="/wiki/My_Country,_%27Tis_of_Thee" title="My Country, &#39;Tis of Thee">My Country, \'Tis of Thee</a>".\n</p><p>Beyond its first verse, which is consistent, "God Save the Queen/King" has many historic and extant versions. Since its first publication, different verses have been added and taken away and, even today, different publications include various selections of verses in various orders.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In general, only one verse is sung. Sometimes two verses are sung, and on rare occasions, three.<sup id="cite_ref-royal.gov.uk_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal.gov.uk-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> and her or his spouse are saluted with the entire composition,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> while other members of the Royal Family who are entitled to royal salute (such as the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cambridge" title="Duke of Cambridge">Duke of Cambridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Sussex" title="Duke of Sussex">Duke of Sussex</a> along with their spouses) receive just the first six bars. The first six bars also form all or part of the <a href="/wiki/Honors_music" title="Honors music">Vice Regal Salute</a> in some Commonwealth realms outside the UK (e.g., in Canada, governors general and lieutenant governors at official events are saluted with the first six bars of "God Save the Queen" followed by the first four and last four bars of "<a href="/wiki/O_Canada" title="O Canada">O Canada</a>"), as well as the salute given to governors of British overseas territories.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" new national anthem/anthems</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The term "God Save the King" historically first appeared in the Hebrew <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>, in the first <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">Books of Kings</a> 1:38\xe2\x80\x9340): \n</p>\n<dl><dd><i><a href="/wiki/Zadok" title="Zadok">Zadok the Priest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nathan_(prophet)" title="Nathan (prophet)">Nathan the Prophet</a> anointed <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a> King.</i></dd>\n<dd><i>And all the people rejoiced, and said:</i></dd>\n<dd><i>God save the King! Long live the King!</i></dd>\n<dd><i>May the King live for ever,</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Amen, <a href="/wiki/Alleluia" title="Alleluia">Alleluia</a>.</i></dd></dl>\n<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oxford_Companion_to_Music" title="The Oxford Companion to Music">The Oxford Companion to Music</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Scholes" title="Percy Scholes">Percy Scholes</a> points out the similarities to an early <a href="/wiki/Plainsong" title="Plainsong">plainsong</a> melody, although the rhythm is very distinctly that of a <a href="/wiki/Galliard" title="Galliard">galliard</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and he gives examples of several such dance tunes that bear a striking resemblance to "God Save the King/Queen". Scholes quotes a keyboard piece by <a href="/wiki/John_Bull_(composer)" title="John Bull (composer)">John Bull</a> (1619) which has some similarities to the modern tune, depending on the placing of <a href="/wiki/Accidental_(music)" title="Accidental (music)">accidentals</a> which at that time were unwritten in certain cases and left to the discretion of the player (see <i><a href="/wiki/Musica_ficta" title="Musica ficta">musica ficta</a></i>). He also points to several pieces by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Henry Purcell</a>, one of which includes the opening notes of the modern tune, setting the words "God Save the King". Nineteenth-century scholars and commentators mention the widespread belief that an old Scots carol, "Remember O Thou Man" was the source of the tune.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first published version of what is almost the present tune appeared in 1744 in <i>Thesaurus Musicus</i>. The 1744 version of the song was popularised in Scotland and England the following year, with the landing of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart" title="Charles Edward Stuart">Charles Edward Stuart</a> and was published in <i>The Gentleman\'s Magazine</i> (see illustration above). This manuscript has the tune depart from that which is used today at several points, one as early as the first bar, but is otherwise clearly a strong relative of the contemporary anthem. It was recorded as being sung in London theatres in 1745, with, for example, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arne" title="Thomas Arne">Thomas Arne</a> writing a setting of the tune for the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">Drury Lane Theatre</a>.\n</p><p>Scholes\' analysis includes mention of "untenable" and "doubtful" claims, as well as "an American misattribution". Some of these are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The French <a href="/wiki/Marquise_de_Cr%C3%A9quy" title="Marquise de Cr\xc3\xa9quy">Marquise de Cr\xc3\xa9quy</a> wrote in her <i>Souvenirs</i> that the words "Grand Dieu, sauvez le Roi!" were indeed composed by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lully" title="Jean-Baptiste Lully">Jean-Baptiste Lully</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> in gratitude for the survival by <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Anal_fistula" title="Anal fistula">anal fistula</a> operation. (The surgical knife purpose-made for the occasion is on display in the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27histoire_de_la_m%C3%A9decine" class="mw-redirect" title="Mus\xc3\xa9e d&#39;histoire de la m\xc3\xa9decine">Mus\xc3\xa9e d\'histoire de la m\xc3\xa9decine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>) Allegedly, Lully had set words by Marie de Brinon to music, and Cr\xc3\xa9quy claimed that the tune was later plagiarised by <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a>, and so adopted by the British as "un de leurs air nationaux". (The entire story might have been intended as a joke.) Translated into Latin under the title <i>Domine, Salvum Fac Regem</i>, it became the French anthem until 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden" title="Battle of Culloden">Battle of Culloden</a>, the Hanover dynasty supposedly adopted this melody as the British anthem.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/James_Oswald_(composer)" title="James Oswald (composer)">James Oswald</a> was a possible author of the <i>Thesaurus Musicus</i>, so may have played a part in the history of the song, but is not a strong enough candidate to be cited as the composer of the tune.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Henry_Carey_(writer)" title="Henry Carey (writer)">Henry Carey</a>: Scholes refutes this attribution: first on the grounds that Carey himself never made such a claim; second, when the claim was made by Carey\'s son (in 1795), it was in support of a request for a pension from the British Government; and third, the younger Carey claimed that his father, who died in 1743, had written parts of the song in 1745. It has also been claimed that the work was first publicly performed by Carey during a dinner in 1740 in honour of Admiral <a href="/wiki/Edward_Vernon" title="Edward Vernon">Edward "Grog" Vernon</a>, who had captured the Spanish harbour of <a href="/wiki/Porto_Bello,_Panama" class="mw-redirect" title="Porto Bello, Panama">Porto Bello</a> (then in <a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia" title="Gran Colombia">Gran Colombia</a>, now in <a href="/wiki/Panam%C3%A1" class="mw-redirect" title="Panam\xc3\xa1">Panam\xc3\xa1</a>) during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Jenkins%27_Ear" title="War of Jenkins&#39; Ear">War of Jenkins\' Ear</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>Scholes recommends the attribution "traditional" or "traditional; earliest known version by John Bull (1562\xe2\x80\x931628)". The <i><a href="/wiki/English_Hymnal" class="mw-redirect" title="English Hymnal">English Hymnal</a></i> (musical editor <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a>) gives no attribution, stating merely "17th or 18th cent."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_lyrics">Original lyrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Original lyrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<blockquote><div class="poem">\n<p>God save great <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George</a> our king<br />\nGod save our noble king,<br />\nGod save the king!<br />\nSend him victorious<br />\nHappy and glorious<br />\nLong to reign over us<br />\nGod save the king!<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (September 2018)">not in citation given</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n</div></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_the_United_Kingdom">Use in the United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Use in the United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/National_anthem_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="National anthem of England">National anthem of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Poster of blimp above London at nighttime, with the text &quot;It is far better to face the bullets than to be killed at home by a bomb. Join the army at once &amp; help to stop an air raid. God save the King&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg/170px-It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg/255px-It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg/340px-It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2685" data-file-height="3968" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The phrase "God Save the King" in use as a rallying cry to the support of the monarch and the UK\'s forces</div></div></div>\n<p>"God Save the Queen" is the <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland. Like many aspects of British constitutional life, its official status derives from custom and use, not from Royal <a href="/wiki/Proclamation" title="Proclamation">Proclamation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a>. In general, only one or two verses are sung, but on rare occasions, three.<sup id="cite_ref-royal.gov.uk_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal.gov.uk-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  The variation in the UK of the lyrics to "God Save the Queen" is the oldest amongst those currently used, and forms the basis on which all other versions used throughout the Commonwealth are formed; though, again, the words have varied throughout these years.\n</p><p>England has no official national anthem of its own; "God Save the Queen" is treated as the English national anthem when England is represented at sporting events (though there are some exceptions to this rule, such as cricket where "<a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">Jerusalem</a>" is used). There is a movement to establish an English national anthem, with <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">Blake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Parry" title="Hubert Parry">Parry</a>\'s "Jerusalem" and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Elgar\'s</a> "<a href="/wiki/Land_of_Hope_and_Glory" title="Land of Hope and Glory">Land of Hope and Glory</a>" among the top contenders. Scotland has its own national song and Wales has its own national anthem for political and national events and for use at international <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> and other sports in which those nations compete independently.<sup id="cite_ref-olympic_usage_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olympic_usage-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> On all occasions Wales\' national anthem is "<a href="/wiki/Hen_Wlad_Fy_Nhadau" title="Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau">Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau</a>" (Land of my Fathers). Scotland has no single anthem; "<a href="/wiki/Scotland_the_Brave" title="Scotland the Brave">Scotland the Brave</a>" was traditionally used until the 1990s, when "<a href="/wiki/Flower_of_Scotland" title="Flower of Scotland">Flower of Scotland</a>" was adopted. In Northern Ireland, "God Save the Queen" is still used as the official anthem.\n</p><p>The phrase "No surrender" is occasionally sung in the bridge before "Send her victorious" by England football fans at matches.<sup id="cite_ref-BackCrabbe2001_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BackCrabbe2001-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The phrase "no surrender" is also associated with <a href="/wiki/Combat_18" title="Combat 18">Combat 18</a>, a white supremacist group.<sup id="cite_ref-IndObit_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndObit-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The phrase is also associated with <a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalism" title="Ulster loyalism">Ulster loyalism</a> and can sometimes be heard at the same point before Northern Ireland football matches.\n</p><p>Since 2003, "God Save the Queen", considered an all inclusive Anthem for Great Britain and Northern Ireland, as well as other countries within the Commonwealth, has been dropped from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>. <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Irish</a> athletes receive their gold medals to the tune of the "<a href="/wiki/Londonderry_Air" title="Londonderry Air">Londonderry Air</a>", popularly known as "<a href="/wiki/Danny_Boy" title="Danny Boy">Danny Boy</a>". In 2006, English winners heard Elgar\'s <a href="/wiki/Pomp_and_Circumstance_Marches" title="Pomp and Circumstance Marches">Pomp and Circumstance March No. 1</a>, usually known as "Land of Hope and Glory",<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> but after a poll conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games_Council_for_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonwealth Games Council for England">Commonwealth Games Council for England</a> prior to the 2010 Games, "Jerusalem" was adopted as England\'s new Commonwealth Games anthem. In sports in which the UK competes as one nation, most notably as <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_at_the_Olympics" title="Great Britain at the Olympics">Great Britain at the Olympics</a>, "God Save the Queen" is used to represent anyone or any team that comes from the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-olympic_usage_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olympic_usage-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyrics_in_the_UK">Lyrics in the UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lyrics in the UK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg/220px-Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg/330px-Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg/440px-Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3136" data-file-height="2498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Town_Hall_Stratford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Town Hall, <a href="/wiki/Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Stratford-upon-Avon">Stratford-upon-Avon</a>, Warwickshire (built 1767), bearing the painted slogan, "God Save the King".</div></div></div>\n<p>The phrase "God Save the King" is much older than the song, appearing, for instance, several times in the <a href="/wiki/King_James_version_of_the_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="King James version of the Bible">King James Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zadok_the_Priest" title="Zadok the Priest">A text</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">1st Book of Kings</a> Chapter 1: verses 38\xe2\x80\x9340, "...And all the people rejoic\'d, and said: God save the King! Long live the King! May the King live for ever, Amen", has been sung at every <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_Monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of the British Monarch">coronation</a> since that of <a href="/wiki/Edgar_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar of England">King Edgar</a> in 973.<sup id="cite_ref-WestminsterAbbey1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WestminsterAbbey1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Scholes says that as early as 1545 "God Save the King" was a <a href="/wiki/Watchword" class="mw-redirect" title="Watchword">watchword</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, with the response being "Long to reign over us".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He also notes that the prayer read in churches on anniversaries of the <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_Plot" title="Gunpowder Plot">Gunpowder Plot</a> includes words which might have formed part of the basis for the second verse "Scatter our enemies...assuage their malice and confound their devices".\n</p><p>In 1745, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gentleman%27s_Magazine" title="The Gentleman&#39;s Magazine">The Gentleman\'s Magazine</a></i> published "God save our lord the king: A new song set for two voices", describing it "As sung at both Playhouses" (the Theatres Royal at <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">Drury Lane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House" title="Royal Opera House">Covent Garden</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally, the first performance was thought to have been in 1745, when it was sung in support of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">King George II</a>, after his defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Prestonpans" title="Battle of Prestonpans">Battle of Prestonpans</a> by the army of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart" title="Charles Edward Stuart">Charles Edward Stuart</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/James_Francis_Edward_Stuart" title="James Francis Edward Stuart">James Francis Edward Stuart</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobite</a> claimant to the British throne.\n</p><p>It is sometimes claimed that, ironically, the song was originally sung in support of the Jacobite cause: the word "send" in the line "Send him victorious" could imply that the king was absent. However, the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> cites examples of "[God] send (a person) safe, victorious, etc." meaning "God grant that he may be safe, etc.". There are also examples of early eighteenth century Jacobean drinking glasses which are inscribed with a version of the words and were apparently intended for drinking the health of <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">King James II and VII</a>.\n</p><p>Scholes acknowledges these possibilities but argues that the same words were probably being used by both Jacobite and Hanoverian supporters and directed at their respective kings.<sup id="cite_ref-mwtppa_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwtppa-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn 1902, the musician <a href="/wiki/William_Hayman_Cummings" title="William Hayman Cummings">William Hayman Cummings</a>, quoting mid-18th century correspondence between <a href="/wiki/Charles_Burney" title="Charles Burney">Charles Burney</a> and Sir Joseph Banks, proposed that the words were based on a Latin verse composed for King James II at the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a>.</p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>O Deus optime<br />\nSalvum nunc facito<br />\nRegem nostrum<br />\nSic laeta victoria<br />\nComes et gloria<br />\nSalvum iam facito<br />\nTu dominum.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_version_in_the_United_Kingdom">Standard version in the United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Standard version in the United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20em;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-title" style="">"God Save the Queen"<br />(standard version)</div>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">\n<div class="poem">\n<p>God save our gracious <b><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen</a></b>!<br />\nLong live our noble <b>Queen</b>!<br />\nGod save the <b>Queen</b>!<br />\nSend <b>her</b> victorious,<br />\nHappy and glorious,<br />\nLong to reign over us:<br />\nGod save the <b>Queen</b>!<br />\n<br />\nO Lord our God arise,<br />\nScatter <b>her</b> enemies,<br />\nAnd make them fall:<br />\nConfound their politics,<br />\nFrustrate their knavish tricks,<br />\nOn Thee our hopes we fix:<br />\nGod save us all.<br />\n<br />\nThy choicest gifts in store,<br />\nOn <b>her</b> be pleased to pour;<br />\nLong may <b>she</b> reign:<br />\nMay <b>she</b> defend our laws,<br />\nAnd ever give us cause,<br />\nTo sing with heart and voice,<br />\nGod save the <b>Queen</b>!<br />\n<br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;"><small>When the monarch of the time is male, "Queen" is replaced with "King" and all feminine pronouns (<b>in bold type</b>) are replaced with their masculine equivalents.</small></span><small><br />\n</small>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>There is no definitive version of the lyrics. However, the version consisting of the three verses reproduced in the box on the right hand side has the best claim to be regarded as the "standard" British version, appearing not only in the 1745 <i>Gentleman\'s Magazine</i>, but also in publications such as <i>The Book of English Songs: From the Sixteenth to the Nineteenth Century</i> (1851),<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <i>National Hymns: How They Are Written and How They Are Not Written</i> (1861),<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <i>Household Book of Poetry</i> (1882),<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Hymns Ancient and Modern, Revised Version</i> (1982).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The same version with verse two omitted appears in publications including <i><a href="/wiki/Scouting_for_Boys" title="Scouting for Boys">Scouting for Boys</a></i> (1908),<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and on the British Monarchy website.<sup id="cite_ref-royal.gov.uk_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal.gov.uk-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> At the Queen\'s <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Jubilee">Golden Jubilee</a> Party at the Palace concert, <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince Charles</a> referred in his speech to the "politically incorrect second verse" of the National Anthem.\n</p><p>According to Alan Michie\'s <i>Rule, Britannia</i>, which was published in 1952, after the death of <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">King George VI</a> but before the coronation of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>, when the first <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">General Assembly of the United Nations</a> was held in London in January 1946 the King, in honour of the occasion, "ordered the belligerent imperious second stanza of \'God Save the King\' to be rewritten to bring it more into the spirit of the brotherhood of nations."\n</p><p>In the UK, the first verse is the only verse typically sung, even at official occasions, although the third verse is sung in addition on rare occasions such as during the opening ceremonies of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a> and <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">2012 Summer Paralympics</a>, and usually at the <a href="/wiki/Last_Night_of_the_Proms" class="mw-redirect" title="Last Night of the Proms">Last Night of the Proms</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics_closing_ceremony" title="2008 Summer Olympics closing ceremony">Closing Ceremony of the 2008 Beijing Olympics</a>, the fourth verse of the <a href="/wiki/William_Edward_Hickson" title="William Edward Hickson">William Hickson</a> alternative lyrics was sung instead of the third verse.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_version_of_the_music">Standard version of the music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Standard version of the music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_1" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:320px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_2&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv/320px--God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv.jpg&quot; controls=&quot;&quot; preload=&quot;none&quot; autoplay=&quot;&quot; style=&quot;width:320px;height:240px&quot; class=&quot;kskin&quot; data-durationhint=&quot;48.133345428157&quot; data-startoffset=&quot;0&quot; data-mwtitle=&quot;God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv&quot; data-mwprovider=&quot;wikimediacommons&quot;&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a9/God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv/God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv.240p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small WebM (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;320&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Lowest bandwidth VP9 (120P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 120P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;120p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;160&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth WebM (160P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 160P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;160p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;214&quot;
