type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 180P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;180p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;240&quot; data-height=&quot;180&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;303752&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30.000300003&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:God Save The Queen 2007 St Giles Fair.ogv" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv/220px--God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:God_Save_The_Queen_2007_St_Giles_Fair.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"God Save the Queen" sung by the public at <a href="/wiki/St_Giles%27_Fair" title="St Giles&#39; Fair">St Giles\' Fair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>, 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>The standard version of the melody and its key of G major are still those of the originally published version, although the start of the anthem is often signalled by an introductory timpani roll of two bars length. The bass line of the standard version differs little from the second voice part shown in the original, and there is a standard version in four-part harmony for choirs. The first three lines (six bars of music) are soft, ending with a short <i>crescendo</i> into "Send her victorious", and then is another <i>crescendo</i> at "over us:" into the final words "God save the Queen".\n</p><p>In the early part of the 20th century there existed a Military Band version in the higher key of B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> because it was easier for brass instruments to play in that key, though it had the disadvantage of being more difficult to sing: however now most Bands play it in the correct key of G.\n</p><p>Since 1953, the anthem is sometimes preceded by a fanfare composed by <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Jacob" title="Gordon Jacob">Gordon Jacob</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">coronation of Queen Elizabeth II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_British_versions">Alternative British versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Alternative British versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There have been several attempts to improve the song by rewriting the words. In the nineteenth century there was some lively debate about the national anthem and, even then, verse two was considered to be slightly offensive. Notably, the question arose over the phrase "scatter her enemies." Some thought it placed better emphasis on the respective power of Parliament and the Crown to change "her" to "our"; others pointed out that the theology was somewhat dubious and substituted "thine" instead. Sydney G. R. Coles wrote a completely new version, as did Canon F. K. Harford.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In 1836, <a href="/wiki/William_Edward_Hickson" title="William Edward Hickson">William Edward Hickson</a> wrote four alternative verses. The first, third, and fourth of these verses are appended to the National Anthem in the <a href="/wiki/English_Hymnal" class="mw-redirect" title="English Hymnal">English Hymnal</a> (which only includes verses one and three of the original lyrics).\n</p>\n<h5><span id="William_Hickson.27s_alternative_version"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="William_Hickson\'s_alternative_version">William Hickson\'s alternative version</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: William Hickson&#039;s alternative version">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>William Hickson\'s alternative (1836) version includes the following verses, of which the first, third, and fourth have some currency as they are appended to the National Anthem in the English Hymnal. The fourth verse was sung after the traditional first verse at the Queen\'s Golden Jubilee National Service of Thanksgiving in 2002 and during the raising of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a> during the closing ceremonies of the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a>.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote">\n<div class="poem">\n<p>God bless our native land!<br />\nMay Heav\'n\'s protecting hand<br />\nStill guard our shore:<br />\nMay peace her power extend,<br />\nFoe be transformed to friend,<br />\nAnd Britain\'s rights depend<br />\nOn war no more.<br />\n<br />\nO Lord, our monarch bless<br />\nWith strength and righteousness:<br />\nLong may she reign:<br />\nHer heart inspire and move<br />\nWith wisdom from above;<br />\nAnd in a nation\'s love<br />\nHer throne maintain<br />\n<br />\nMay just and righteous laws<br />\nUphold the public cause,<br />\nAnd bless our Isle:<br />\nHome of the brave and free,<br />\nThou land of Liberty,<br />\nWe pray that still on thee<br />\nKind Heav\'n may smile.<br />\n<br />\nNot in this land alone,<br />\nBut be God\'s mercies known<br />\nFrom shore to shore:<br />\nLord make the nations see<br />\nThat men should brothers be,<br />\nAnd form one family<br />\nThe wide world o\'er\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_peace_version">Official peace version</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Official peace version">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>A less militaristic version of the song, titled "Official peace version, 1919", was first published in the <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a> book <i>Songs of Praise</i> in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This was "official" in the sense that it was approved by the <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Honourable_Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Most Honourable Privy Council">British Privy Council</a> in 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-mwtppa_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwtppa-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> However, despite being reproduced in some other <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a> books, it is largely unknown today.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote">\n<div class="poem">\n<p>God save our gracious Queen!<br />\nLong live our noble Queen!<br />\nGod save The Queen!<br />\nSend her victorious<br />\nHappy and glorious<br />\nLong to reign over us<br />\nGod save the Queen!<br />\n<br />\nOne realm of races four<br />\nBlest more and ever more<br />\nGod save our land!<br />\nHome of the brave and free<br />\nSet in the silver sea<br />\nTrue nurse of chivalry<br />\nGod save our land!<br />\n<br />\nOf many a race and birth<br />\nFrom utmost ends of earth<br />\nGod save us all!<br />\nBid strife and hatred cease<br />\nBid hope and joy increase<br />\nSpread universal peace<br />\nGod save us all!\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_Jacobite_and_anti-Jacobite_alternative_verses">Historic Jacobite and anti-Jacobite alternative verses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Historic Jacobite and anti-Jacobite alternative verses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Around 1745, anti-<a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobite</a> sentiment was captured in a verse appended to the song, with a prayer for the success of <a href="/wiki/Field_Marshal_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Field Marshal (United Kingdom)">Field Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/George_Wade" title="George Wade">George Wade</a>\'s army then assembling at <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a>. These words attained some short-term use, although they did not appear in the published version in the October 1745 <i>Gentleman\'s Magazine</i>. This verse was first documented as an occasional addition to the original anthem by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Clark_(musician)" title="Richard Clark (musician)">Richard Clark</a> in 1822,<sup id="cite_ref-clark_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clark-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and was also mentioned in a later article on the song, published by the <i>Gentleman\'s Magazine</i> in October 1836. Therein, it is presented as an "additional verse... though being of temporary application only... stored in the memory of an old friend... who was born in the very year 1745, and was thus the associate of those who heard it first sung", the lyrics given being:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>Lord, grant that Marshal Wade,<br />\nMay by thy mighty aid,<br />\nVictory bring.<br />\nMay he sedition hush,<br />\nand like a torrent rush,<br />\nRebellious Scots to crush,<br />\nGod save the King.\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The 1836 article and other sources make it clear that this verse was not used soon after 1745, and certainly before the song became accepted as the British national anthem in the 1780s and 1790s.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> It was included as an integral part of the song in the <i>Oxford Book of Eighteenth Century Verse</i> of 1926, although erroneously referencing the "fourth verse" to the <i>Gentleman\'s Magazine</i> article of 1745.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the opposing side, Jacobite beliefs were demonstrated in an alternative verse used during the same period:<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>God bless the prince, I pray,<br />\nGod bless the prince, I pray,<br />\n<a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart" title="Charles Edward Stuart">Charlie</a> I mean;<br />\nThat Scotland we may see<br />\nFreed from vile <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyt\'ry</a>,<br />\nBoth <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Frederick,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Frederick, Prince of Wales">Feckie</a>,<br />\nEver so, Amen.\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>In May 1800, following an attempt to assassinate <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a> at London\'s Drury Lane theatre, playwright <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brinsley_Sheridan" title="Richard Brinsley Sheridan">Richard Sheridan</a> immediately composed an additional verse, which was sung from the stage the same night:<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>From every latent foe<br />\nFrom the assassin\'s blow<br />\nGod save the King<br />\nO\'er him Thine arm extend<br />\nFor Britain\'s sake defend<br />\nOur father, king, and friend<br />\nGod save the King!\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Various other attempts were made during the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries to add verses to commemorate particular royal or national events. For example, according to Fitzroy Maclean, when Jacobite forces bypassed Wade\'s force and reached <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a>, but then retreated and when their garrison at <a href="/wiki/Carlisle_Castle" title="Carlisle Castle">Carlisle Castle</a> surrendered to a second government army led by King George\'s son, the <a href="/wiki/William_Augustus,_Duke_of_Cumberland" class="mw-redirect" title="William Augustus, Duke of Cumberland">Duke of Cumberland</a>, another verse was added.<sup id="cite_ref-fn_1_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fn_1-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Other short-lived verses were notably anti-French, such as the following, quoted in the book <i>Handel</i> by Edward J. Dent:<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>From France and Pretender<br />\nGreat Britain defend her,<br />\nFoes let them fall;<br />\nFrom foreign slavery,<br />\nPriests and their knavery,<br />\nAnd Popish Reverie,<br />\nGod save us all.\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>However, none of these additional verses survived into the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Updated "full" versions including additional verses have been published more recently, including the standard three verses, Hickson\'s fourth verse, Sheridan\'s verse and the Marshal Wade verse.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_republican_alternative">Historic republican alternative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Historic republican alternative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A version from 1794 composed by the American republican and French citizen <a href="/wiki/Joel_Barlow" title="Joel Barlow">Joel Barlow</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> celebrated the power of the <a href="/wiki/Guillotine" title="Guillotine">guillotine</a> to liberate:<sup id="cite_ref-poetrywars_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poetrywars-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>God save the Guillotine<br />\nTill England\xe2\x80\x99s King and Queen<br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">Her power shall prove:</span><br />\nTill each appointed knob<br />\nAffords a clipping job<br />\nLet no vile halter rob<br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;"><i>The Guillotine</i></span><br />\n<br />\nFrance, let thy trumpet sound \xe2\x80\x93 <br />\nTell all the world around <br />\nHow <a href="/wiki/House_of_Capet" title="House of Capet">CAPET</a> fell; <br />\nAnd when great <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">GEORGE</a>\'s poll <br />\nShall in the basket roll, <br />\nLet mercy then control <br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;"><i>The Guillotine</i><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></span><br />\n<br />\nWhen all the sceptre\'d crew<br />\nHave paid their Homage, due<br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;"><i>The Guillotine</i></span><br />\nLet Freedom\xe2\x80\x99s flag advance<br />\nTill all the world, like France<br />\nO\'er tyrants\' graves shall dance<br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">And PEACE begin.</span>\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_in_the_UK">Performance in the UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Performance in the UK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The style most commonly heard in official performances was proposed as the "proper interpretation" by King <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a>, who considered himself something of an expert (in view of the number of times he had heard it). An Army Order was duly issued in 1933, which laid down regulations for tempo, dynamics and orchestration. This included instructions such as that the opening "six bars will be played quietly by the reed band with horns and basses in a single phrase. Cornets and side-drum are to be added at the little scale-passage leading into the second half of the tune, and the full brass enters for the last eight bars". The official tempo for the opening section is a metronome setting of 60, with the second part played in a broader manner, at a metronome setting of 52.<sup id="cite_ref-fn_2_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fn_2-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In recent years the prescribed sombre-paced introduction is often played at a faster and livelier <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">tempo</a>.\n</p><p>Until the latter part of the 20th century, theatre and concert goers were expected to stand while the anthem was played after the conclusion of a show. In cinemas this brought a tendency for audiences to rush out while the end credits played to avoid this formality.  (This can be seen in the 1972 <i><a href="/wiki/Dad%27s_Army" title="Dad&#39;s Army">Dad\'s Army</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/A_Soldier%27s_Farewell" title="A Soldier&#39;s Farewell">A Soldier\'s Farewell</a>".)\n</p><p>The anthem continues to be played at some traditional events such as <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Variety_Performance" title="Royal Variety Performance">Royal Variety Performance</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Tattoo" class="mw-redirect" title="Edinburgh Tattoo">Edinburgh Tattoo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ascot" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Ascot">Royal Ascot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henley_Royal_Regatta" title="Henley Royal Regatta">Henley Royal Regatta</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Proms" title="The Proms">The Proms</a> as well as at Royal events.\n</p><p>The anthem was traditionally played at closedown on the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, and with the introduction of commercial television to the UK this practice was adopted by some <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_channel)" title="ITV (TV channel)">ITV</a> companies (with the notable exception of <a href="/wiki/Granada_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Granada Television">Granada</a>). <a href="/wiki/BBC_Two" title="BBC Two">BBC Two</a> never played the anthem at closedown, and ITV dropped the practice in the late 1980s, but it continued on <a href="/wiki/BBC_One" title="BBC One">BBC One</a> until the final closedown on 8 November 1997 (thereafter BBC One began to <a href="/wiki/Simulcast" title="Simulcast">simulcast</a> with <a href="/wiki/BBC_News" title="BBC News">BBC News</a> after end of programmes).  The tradition is carried on, however, by <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">BBC Radio 4</a>, which plays the anthem each night as a transition piece between the end of the Radio 4 broadcasting and the move to <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC World Service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_on_Radio_4_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_on_Radio_4-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Radio 4 and <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_2" title="BBC Radio 2">Radio 2</a> also play the National Anthem at 0700 and 0800 on the actual and <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Official_Birthday" title="Queen&#39;s Official Birthday">official</a> birthdays of the Queen and the birthdays of senior members of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Family">Royal Family</a>.\n</p><p>The UK\'s national anthem usually prefaces <a href="/wiki/Royal_Christmas_Message" title="Royal Christmas Message">The Queen\'s Christmas Message</a> (although in 2007 it appeared at the end, taken from a recording of the 1957 television broadcast), and important royal announcements, such as of royal deaths, when it is played in a slower, sombre arrangement.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_British_anthems">Other British anthems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other British anthems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Frequently, when an anthem is needed for one of the constituent <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32; at an international sporting event, for instance&#160;&#8211;&#32; an alternative song is used:\n</p>\n<ul><li>England generally uses "God Save the Queen", but "<a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">Jerusalem</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Rule,_Britannia!" title="Rule, Britannia!">Rule, Britannia!</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Land_of_Hope_and_Glory" title="Land of Hope and Glory">Land of Hope and Glory</a>" have also been used.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n<ul><li>At international <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">test cricket</a> matches, <a href="/wiki/England_cricket_team" title="England cricket team">England</a> has, since 2004, used "<a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">Jerusalem</a>" as the anthem.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>At international <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a> matches, <a href="/wiki/England_national_rugby_league_team" title="England national rugby league team">England</a> uses "God Save the Queen" and also "Jerusalem".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>At international <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> matches, England uses "God Save the Queen".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>At the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games_England" title="Commonwealth Games England">Team England</a> uses "Jerusalem" as their victory anthem.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> uses "<a href="/wiki/Flower_of_Scotland" title="Flower of Scotland">Flower of Scotland</a>" as their anthem for most sporting occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> uses <i><a href="/wiki/Hen_Wlad_Fy_Nhadau" title="Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau">Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau</a></i> ("Land of My Fathers") for governmental ceremonies and sporting occasions. At official occasions, especially those with royal connections, "God Save the Queen" is also played.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> uses "God Save the Queen" as its national anthem. Northern Ireland also uses "<a href="/wiki/Londonderry_Air" title="Londonderry Air">Londonderry Air</a>" as its victory anthem at the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However, many <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">Irish nationalists</a> feel unrepresented by the unionist anthem and seek an alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> At international <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> matches, where Northern Irish players compete alongside those from the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> as part of an <a href="/wiki/All-Ireland" title="All-Ireland">All-Ireland</a> team, "<a href="/wiki/Ireland%27s_Call" title="Ireland&#39;s Call">Ireland\'s Call</a>" is used.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/British_and_Irish_Lions" title="British and Irish Lions">British and Irish Lions</a> rugby union tour used the song "<a href="/wiki/The_Power_of_Four" title="The Power of Four">The Power of Four</a>", but this anthem was especially designed for the 2005 tour and was used only then.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>In April 2007 there was an <a href="/wiki/Early_day_motion" title="Early day motion">early day motion</a>, number 1319, to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British Parliament</a> to propose that there should be a separate England anthem: "That this House ... believes that all English sporting associations should adopt an appropriate song that English sportsmen and women, and the English public, would favour when competing as England". An amendment (EDM 1319A3) was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Evan_Harris" title="Evan Harris">Evan Harris</a> that the song "should have a bit more oomph than <i>God Save The Queen</i> and should also not involve God."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For more information see also:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Proposals_for_a_national_anthem_for_England" title="Proposals for a national anthem for England">National anthem of England</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/National_anthem_of_Scotland" title="National anthem of Scotland">National anthem of Scotland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hen_Wlad_Fy_Nhadau" title="Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau">Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Londonderry_Air" title="Londonderry Air">Londonderry Air</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_other_Commonwealth_countries">Use in other Commonwealth countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Use in other Commonwealth countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>"God Save the King/Queen" was exported around the world via the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, serving as each country\'s national anthem. Throughout the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations#History" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Empire\'s evolution</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, the song declined in use in most states which became independent. In some countries it remains as one of the official national anthems, such as in New Zealand,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> or as an official <a href="/wiki/Royal_anthem" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal anthem">royal anthem</a>, as is the case in Australia, Canada, Jamaica, and Tuvalu, to be played during formal ceremonies involving national royalty or vice-royalty.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Australian_royal_symbols#Verbal_and_musical_symbols" title="Australian royal symbols">Australian royal symbols \xc2\xa7&#160;Verbal and musical symbols</a></div>\n<p>In Australia, the song has standing through a Royal Proclamation issued by Governor-General <a href="/wiki/Ninian_Stephen" title="Ninian Stephen">Sir Ninian Stephen</a> on 19 April 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It declared "God Save the Queen" to be the Royal Anthem and that it is to be played when the Australian monarch or a member of the Royal Family is present, though not exclusively in such circumstances. The same proclamation made "<a href="/wiki/Advance_Australia_Fair" title="Advance Australia Fair">Advance Australia Fair</a>" the national anthem and the basis for the "Vice-Regal Salute" (the first four and last two bars of the anthem). Prior to 1984, "God Save the Queen" was the national anthem of Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 1975 former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Gough_Whitlam" title="Gough Whitlam">Gough Whitlam</a>, dismissed by <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Australia" title="Governor-General of Australia">Governor-General</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Kerr_(governor-general)" title="John Kerr (governor-general)">Sir John Kerr</a>, alluded to the anthem in his comment "Well may we say \'God save the Queen\', because nothing will save the Governor-General!".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_royal_symbols#Verbal_and_musical_symbols" title="Canadian royal symbols">Canadian royal symbols \xc2\xa7&#160;Verbal and musical symbols</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anthems_and_nationalistic_songs_of_Canada" title="Anthems and nationalistic songs of Canada">Anthems and nationalistic songs of Canada</a></div>\n<p>By <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention (norm)">convention</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DCH_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCH-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> "God Save the Queen" (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Dieu Sauve la Reine</i>) is the Royal Anthem of Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CE_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> It is sometimes played or sung together with the <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a>, "<a href="/wiki/O_Canada" title="O Canada">O Canada</a>", at private and public events organised by groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Canada" title="Government of Canada">Government of Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Legion" title="Royal Canadian Legion">Royal Canadian Legion</a>, police services, and loyal groups.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The governor general and provincial lieutenant governors are accorded the "Viceregal Salute", comprising the first three lines of "God Save the Queen", followed by the first and last lines of "O Canada".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>"God Save the Queen" has been sung in Canada since the late 1700s and by the mid 20th century was, along with "O Canada", one of the country\'s two <i>de facto</i> national anthems, the first and last verses of the standard British version being used.<sup id="cite_ref-QHE_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QHE-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> By-laws and practices governing the use of either song during public events in municipalities varied; in Toronto, "God Save the Queen" was employed, while in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> it was "O Canada". Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Lester_B._Pearson" title="Lester B. Pearson">Lester B. Pearson</a> in 1964 said one song would have to be chosen as the country\'s national anthem and, three years later, he advised <a href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_Canada" title="Governor General of Canada">Governor General</a> <a href="/wiki/Georges_Vanier" title="Georges Vanier">Georges Vanier</a> to appoint the Special Joint Committee of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Canada" title="Senate of Canada">Senate</a> and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_Canada" title="House of Commons of Canada">House of Commons</a> on the National and Royal Anthems. Within two months, on 12 April 1967, the committee presented its conclusion that "God Save the Queen", whose music and lyrics were found to be in the <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">public domain</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DCHOCanada_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCHOCanada-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> should be designated as the Royal Anthem of Canada and "O Canada" as the national anthem, one verse from each, in <a href="/wiki/Official_bilingualism_in_Canada" title="Official bilingualism in Canada">both official languages</a>, to be adopted by parliament. The group was then charged with establishing official lyrics for each song; for "God Save the Queen", the English words were those inherited from the United Kingdom and the French words were taken from those that had been adopted in 1952 for the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Coronation of Elizabeth II">coronation of Elizabeth II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CE_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> When the bill pronouncing "O Canada" as the national anthem was put through parliament, the joint committee\'s earlier recommendations regarding "God Save the Queen" were not included.<sup id="cite_ref-DCHOCanada_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCHOCanada-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Department_of_National_Defence_(Canada)" title="Department of National Defence (Canada)">Department of National Defence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Forces">Canadian Forces</a> regulates that "God Save the Queen" be played as a salute to <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Canada" title="Monarchy of Canada">the monarch</a> and other members of the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Canada#Canadian_Royal_Family" title="Monarchy of Canada">Canadian Royal Family</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DND_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DND-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> though it may also be used as a hymn, or prayer. The words are not to be sung when the song is played as a military royal salute and is abbreviated to the first three lines while arms are being presented.<sup id="cite_ref-DND_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DND-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> stipulated that the arrangement in G major by Lieutenant Colonel Basil H. Brown be used in Canada. The authorised version to be played by <a href="/wiki/Pipe_band" title="Pipe band">pipe bands</a> is <i>Mallorca</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-DND_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DND-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyrics_in_Canada">Lyrics in Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Lyrics in Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The first verse of "God Save the Queen" has been translated into French,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> as shown below:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Dieu prot\xc3\xa8ge la reine</dd>\n<dd>Proteg\'ra souveraine!</dd>\n<dd>Vive la reine!</dd>\n<dd>Qu\'un r\xc3\xa8gne glorieux</dd>\n<dd>Long et victorieux,</dd>\n<dd>Rende son peuple heureux.</dd>\n<dd>Vive la reine!</dd></dl>\n<p>There is a special Canadian verse in English which was once commonly sung in addition to the two standing verses:<sup id="cite_ref-QHE_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QHE-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Our loved Dominion bless</dd>\n<dd>With peace and happiness</dd>\n<dd>From shore to shore;</dd>\n<dd>And let our Empire be</dd>\n<dd>Loyal, united, free,</dd>\n<dd>True to herself and Thee</dd>\n<dd>For evermore.</dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>"God Save the Queen" was the sole official national anthem until 1977 when "<a href="/wiki/God_Defend_New_Zealand" title="God Defend New Zealand">God Defend New Zealand</a>" was added as a second. "God Save the Queen" is now most often only played when the sovereign, <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_New_Zealand" title="Governor-General of New Zealand">governor-general</a><sup id="cite_ref-cryer_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cryer-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> or other member of the Royal Family is present, or on some occasions such as <a href="/wiki/Anzac_Day" title="Anzac Day">Anzac Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AnthemProtocol_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AnthemProtocol-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In New Zealand, the second more militaristic verse is sometimes replaced with Hickson\'s verse "Nor in this land alone..." (often sung as "<i>Not</i> in this land alone"), otherwise known as a "Commonwealth verse".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rhodesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a> issued its <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia&#39;s Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> from the UK on 11 November 1965, it did so while still maintaining loyalty to Queen Elizabeth II as the Rhodesian head of state, despite the non-recognition of the Rhodesian government by the United Kingdom and the United Nations;<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> "God Save the Queen" therefore remained the Rhodesian national anthem. This was supposed to demonstrate the continued allegiance of the Rhodesian people to the monarch, but the retention in Rhodesia of a song so associated with the UK while the two countries were at loggerheads regarding its constitutional status caused Rhodesian state occasions to have "a faintly ironic tone", in the words of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>. Nevertheless, "God Save the Queen" remained Rhodesia\'s national anthem until March 1970, when the country formally declared itself a republic.<sup id="cite_ref-buch243_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buch243-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Rise,_O_Voices_of_Rhodesia" title="Rise, O Voices of Rhodesia">Rise, O Voices of Rhodesia</a>" was adopted in its stead in 1974 and remained in use until the country returned to British control in December 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Since the internationally recognised independence of the Republic of <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> in April 1980, "God Save the Queen" has had no official status there.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_elsewhere">Use elsewhere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Use elsewhere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>"God Save the King" was one of the first songs to successfully be used as a national anthem; only the Dutch <i><a href="/wiki/Het_Wilhelmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Het Wilhelmus">Het Wilhelmus</a></i> (~1568) is older.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> (The Spanish <i><a href="/wiki/La_Marcha_Real" class="mw-redirect" title="La Marcha Real">La Marcha Real</a></i> is older as well, but took longer to become popular. Japan\'s anthem <i><a href="/wiki/Kimigayo" title="Kimigayo">Kimigayo</a></i> has lyrics which are older still, but a more recent melody). Its success prompted a number of other countries to pen similar anthems to help construct a concrete national identity - many of which used the same tune:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The Imperial anthem of <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Heil_dir_im_Siegerkranz" title="Heil dir im Siegerkranz">Heil dir im Siegerkranz</a></i> (originally the royal anthem of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a> since 1795), used the melody of "God Save the King". In addition, several <a href="/wiki/States_of_the_German_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="States of the German Empire">German states</a> used the tune, including <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bavaria" title="Kingdom of Bavaria">Bavaria</a> (<i>Heil unserm K\xc3\xb6nig, Heil! - Hail to our King, Hail!</i>), <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saxony" title="Kingdom of Saxony">Saxony</a> (<i>Gott segne Sachsenland - God bless Saxony</i>) and Liechtenstein, which did not join the German Empire (see below). During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">Otto of Greece</a>, the Bavarian royal anthem also served as the Greek anthem.</li>\n<li>The anthem of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Kingdom of Hanover</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Heil_dir,_Hannover" title="Heil dir, Hannover">Heil dir, Hannover</a></i>, used the melody of "God Save the King". Hanover was in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> with the Kingdom of Great Britain and subsequently the United Kingdom from 1714 to 1837.</li>\n<li>The national anthem of <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Imperial Russia</a> from 1816 to 1833 was <i><a href="/wiki/Molitva_russkikh" class="mw-redirect" title="Molitva russkikh">Molitva russkikh</a></i> ("The Prayer of Russians"), which used the melody of "God Save the King" and lyrics by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Zhukovsky" title="Vasily Zhukovsky">Vasily Zhukovsky</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>In Switzerland (<i><a href="/wiki/Rufst_Du,_mein_Vaterland" class="mw-redirect" title="Rufst Du, mein Vaterland">Rufst Du, mein Vaterland</a></i> or <i>\xc3\x94 monts ind\xc3\xa9pendants</i>, until 1961).</li>\n<li>"God Save the King" was used as the national anthem of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Kingdom of Hawaii</a> before 1860</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/E_Ola_Ke_Alii_Ke_Akua" title="E Ola Ke Alii Ke Akua">E Ola Ke Alii Ke Akua</a>, from 1860 to 1886 the national anthem of Hawaii, was set to the same melody.</li>\n<li>The Hawaiian anthem <a href="/wiki/Hawai%CA%BBi_Pono%CA%BB%C4%AB" title="Hawai\xca\xbbi Pono\xca\xbb\xc4\xab">Hawai\xca\xbbi Pono\xca\xbb\xc4\xab</a> composed by the Prussian <a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a> <a href="/wiki/Henri_Berger" title="Henri Berger">Henri Berger</a> is a variation of the melody.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The royal and national anthem of the <a href="/wiki/Rattanakosin_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Rattanakosin Kingdom">Kingdom of Siam</a> <i>"<a href="/wiki/Thai_National_Anthem" title="Thai National Anthem">Chomrat Chongcharoen</a>"</i>  used the melody of "God Save the King" from 1852 to 1871 in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Rama_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Rama IV">Rama IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rama_V" class="mw-redirect" title="Rama V">Rama V</a> before adoption of "<a href="/wiki/Sansoen_Phra_Barami" title="Sansoen Phra Barami">Sansoen Phra Barami</a>" in 1888</li>\n<li>The American patriotic <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a> "<a href="/wiki/My_Country,_%27Tis_of_Thee" title="My Country, &#39;Tis of Thee">My Country, \'Tis of Thee</a>", the lyrics of which were written by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Francis_Smith" title="Samuel Francis Smith">Samuel Francis Smith</a> in 1831. The song is often quoted \xe2\x80\x93 alongside "<a href="/wiki/Hail,_Columbia" title="Hail, Columbia">Hail, Columbia</a>" \xe2\x80\x93 as a <i>de facto</i> national anthem for the United States, before the <i>de jure</i> adoption of "<a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">The Star-Spangled Banner</a>" in 1931.</li>\n<li>Norway\'s <a href="/wiki/Royal_anthem" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal anthem">royal anthem</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Kongesangen" title="Kongesangen">Kongesangen</a></i> uses the melody.</li>\n<li>The Swedish royal anthem <i><a href="/wiki/Bevare_Gud_v%C3%A5r_kung" title="Bevare Gud v\xc3\xa5r kung">Bevare Gud v\xc3\xa5r kung</a></i> between 1805 and 1880, used the melody.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>\'s anthem <i><a href="/wiki/Oben_am_jungen_Rhein" title="Oben am jungen Rhein">Oben am jungen Rhein</a></i> uses the same melody. In consequence, the tune was played twice before a <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Football_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="2004 European Football Championship">Euro 2004</a> qualifying match between <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_national_football_team" title="Liechtenstein national football team">Liechtenstein</a>; and again before the <a href="/wiki/Euro_96" class="mw-redirect" title="Euro 96">Euro 96</a> qualifier between <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_national_football_team" title="Northern Ireland national football team">Northern Ireland</a> and Liechtenstein. (When England plays Northern Ireland, the tune is only played once).</li>\n<li>Iceland\'s de facto national anthem in the 19th century was <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%8Dslands_minni&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="\xc3\x8dslands minni (page does not exist)">\xc3\x8dslands minni</a></i> ("To Iceland", better known as <i>Eldgamla \xc3\x8dsafold</i>), a poem by <a href="/wiki/Bjarni_Thorarensen" title="Bjarni Thorarensen">Bjarni Thorarensen</a><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> set to the melody of "God Save the King". This lasted until the <a href="/wiki/Lofs%C3%B6ngur" title="Lofs\xc3\xb6ngur">current national anthem</a> was adopted, first by popular consent and later by law. The tune remains a popular one in Iceland and many different texts\xe2\x80\x94serious, satirical and comical\xe2\x80\x94have been set to it.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece" title="Kingdom of Greece">Greece</a> adopted the melody as its national anthem during the autocratic rule of <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">Otto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Based on the German version, it had adapted lyrics. It was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Hymn_to_Liberty" title="Hymn to Liberty">Hymn to Liberty</a> in 1864, following Otto\'s overthrow.</li></ul>\n<p>The melody is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Hymn_tune" title="Hymn tune">hymn tune</a> by Christian churches in various countries, including by the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> and other denominations. "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110816030738/http://www.hymntime.com/tch/htm/g/g/o/ggodhigh.htm">Glory to God on High</a>" is frequently sung to the tune, as is "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://blog.daum.net/yhdvr/8905694">Since I Have My Retreat"</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Korea" title="Christianity in Korea">Protestant Church of Korea</a>, and the Dutch hymn "Eeuwig en machtig Heer".\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Ren_%26_Stimpy_Show" title="The Ren &amp; Stimpy Show">The Ren &amp; Stimpy Show</a></i> uses a parody version of the tune for the anthem of the "Royal Canadian Kilted Yaksmen".\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_adaptations">Musical adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Musical adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composers">Composers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Composers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>About 140 composers have used the tune in their compositions, including Beethoven, Haydn, Brahms,<sup id="cite_ref-royal.gov.uk_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal.gov.uk-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Clementi, J. C. Bach, Liszt, Britten, Carl Maria von Weber, Niccol\xc3\xb2 Paganini, Johann Strauss I, and Edward Elgar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> composed a set of seven piano variations in the key of C major to the theme of "God Save the King", catalogued as <a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a>.78 (1802\xe2\x80\x931803). He also quotes it in his orchestral work <i><a href="/wiki/Wellington%27s_Victory" title="Wellington&#39;s Victory">Wellington\'s Victory</a></i>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Muzio_Clementi" title="Muzio Clementi">Muzio Clementi</a> used the theme to "God Save the King" in his <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">Symphony</a> No. 3 in G major</i>, often called the "Great National Symphony", catalogued as <a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a>. 34. Clementi paid a high tribute to his adopted homeland (the United Kingdom) where he grew up and stayed most of his lifetime. He based the Symphony (about 1816\xe2\x80\x931824) on "God Save the King", which is hinted at earlier in the work, not least in the second movement, and announced by the trombones in the finale.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Symphony No. 3 "Great National Symphony " in en sol majeur/G-dur/G major/sol maggiore\n1. Andante sostenuto \xe2\x80\x93 Allegro con brio\n2. Andante un poco mosso\n3. Minuetto. Allegretto\n4. Finale. Vivace\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Bach" title="Johann Christian Bach">Johann Christian Bach</a> composed a set of variations on "God Save the King" for the finale to his sixth keyboard concerto (Op. 1) written c. 1763.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> was impressed by the use of "God Save the King" as a national anthem during his visit to London in 1794, and on his return to Austria composed "<a href="/wiki/Gott_erhalte_Franz_den_Kaiser" title="Gott erhalte Franz den Kaiser">Gott erhalte Franz den Kaiser</a>" ("God Save Emperor Francis") for the birthday of the last <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">Roman-German King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Francis II, Holy Roman Emperor">Francis II</a>. It became the anthem of the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a> after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> with revised lyrics, its tune ultimately being used for the <a href="/wiki/German_national_anthem" class="mw-redirect" title="German national anthem">German national anthem</a>. The tune of "God Save the King" was adopted for the Prussian royal anthem "<a href="/wiki/Heil_Dir_im_Siegerkranz" class="mw-redirect" title="Heil Dir im Siegerkranz">Heil Dir im Siegerkranz</a>".\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a> wrote a piano paraphrase on the anthem (S.259 in the official catalogue, c. 1841).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Strauss_I" title="Johann Strauss I">Johann Strauss I</a> quoted "God Save the Queen" in full at the end of his <a href="/wiki/Waltz" title="Waltz">waltz</a> <i>Huldigung der K\xc3\xb6nigin Victoria von Grossbritannien</i> (Homage to Queen Victoria of Great Britain) Op. 103, where he also quoted <i><a href="/wiki/Rule,_Britannia!" title="Rule, Britannia!">Rule, Britannia!</a></i> in full at the beginning of the piece.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Siegfried_August_Mahlmann" title="Siegfried August Mahlmann">Siegfried August Mahlmann</a> in the early 19th century wrote alternate lyrics to adapt the hymn for the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saxony" title="Kingdom of Saxony">Kingdom of Saxony</a>, as "Gott segne Sachsenland" ("God Bless Saxony").<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Marschner" title="Heinrich Marschner">Heinrich Marschner</a> used the anthem in his "Grande Ouverture solenne", op.78 (1842).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Donizetti" title="Gaetano Donizetti">Gaetano Donizetti</a> used this anthem in his opera "<a href="/wiki/Roberto_Devereux" title="Roberto Devereux">Roberto Devereux</a>".\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joachim_Raff" title="Joachim Raff">Joachim Raff</a> used this anthem in his Jubelouverture, Opus 103 (1864) dedicated to Adolf, Herzog von Nassau, on the 25th anniversary of his reign.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gioachino_Rossini" title="Gioachino Rossini">Gioachino Rossini</a> used this anthem in the last scene of his "<a href="/wiki/Il_viaggio_a_Reims" title="Il viaggio a Reims">Il viaggio a Reims</a>", when all the characters, coming from many different European countries, sing a song which recalls their own homeland. Lord Sidney, bass, sings "Della real pianta" on the notes of "God Save the King". <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Ramey" title="Samuel Ramey">Samuel Ramey</a> used to interpolate a spectacular virtuoso <a href="/wiki/Cadenza" title="Cadenza">cadenza</a> at the end of the song.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fernando_Sor" title="Fernando Sor">Fernando Sor</a> used the anthem in his 12 Studies, Op. 6: No. 10 in C Major in the section marked \'Maestoso.\'\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan" title="Arthur Sullivan">Arthur Sullivan</a> quotes the anthem at the end of his ballet <i><a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Merrie_England" title="Victoria and Merrie England">Victoria and Merrie England</a></i>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Claude_Debussy" title="Claude Debussy">Claude Debussy</a> opens with a brief introduction of "God Save the King" in one of his <a href="/wiki/Preludes_(Debussy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Preludes (Debussy)">Preludes</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hommage_%C3%A0_S._Pickwick_Esq._P.P.M.P.C." title="Hommage \xc3\xa0 S. Pickwick Esq. P.P.M.P.C.">Hommage \xc3\xa0 S. Pickwick Esq. P.P.M.P.C.</a></i>. The piece draws its inspiration from the main character of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pickwick_Papers" title="The Pickwick Papers">The Pickwick Papers</a></i>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Paganini" title="Niccol\xc3\xb2 Paganini">Niccol\xc3\xb2 Paganini</a> wrote a set of highly virtuosic variations on "God Save the King" as his Opus 9.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Max_Reger" title="Max Reger">Max Reger</a> wrote <i>Variations and Fugue on \'Heil dir im Siegerkranz\' (God Save the King)</i> for organ in 1901 after the death of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>. It does not have an opus number.\n</p><p>A week before the Coronation Ode was due to be premiered at the June 1902 "Coronation Gala Concert" at <a href="/wiki/Covent_Garden" title="Covent Garden">Covent Garden</a> (it was cancelled, owing to the King\'s illness), <a href="/wiki/Sir_Edward_Elgar" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Edward Elgar">Sir Edward Elgar</a> introduced an arrangement of "Land of Hope and Glory" as a solo song performed by Clara Butt at a "Coronation Concert" at the Albert Hall. Novello seized upon the prevailing patriotism and requested that Elgar arrange the National Anthem as an appropriate opening for a concert performed in front of the Court and numerous British and foreign dignitaries. This version for orchestra and chorus, which is enlivened by use of <i>a cappella</i> and marcato effects, was also performed at the opening of the British Empire Exhibition at Wembley on St. George\xe2\x80\x99s Day, 1924, and recorded under the composer\'s Baton in 1928, with the LSO and the Philharmonic Choir.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Elgar also used the first verse of the Anthem as the climax of a short "Civic Procession and Anthem", written to accompany the mayoral procession at the opening of the Hereford Music Festival on 4 September 1927. This premiere performance was recorded, and is today available on CD; the score was lost following the festival, and Elgar resorted to reconstructing it by ear from the recording.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carl_Maria_von_Weber" title="Carl Maria von Weber">Carl Maria von Weber</a> uses the "God Save the King" theme at the end of his "Jubel Overture".\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Verdi" title="Giuseppe Verdi">Giuseppe Verdi</a> included "God Save the Queen" in his "<a href="/wiki/Inno_delle_nazioni" title="Inno delle nazioni">Inno delle nazioni</a>" (Hymn of the Nations), composed for the London <a href="/wiki/1862_International_Exhibition" title="1862 International Exhibition">1862 International Exhibition</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Britten" title="Benjamin Britten">Benjamin Britten</a> arranged "God Save the Queen" in 1961 for the <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Festival_(classical_music)" title="Leeds Festival (classical music)">Leeds Festival</a>. This version has been programmed several times at the <a href="/wiki/Last_Night_of_the_Proms" class="mw-redirect" title="Last Night of the Proms">Last Night of the Proms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Ives" title="Charles Ives">Charles Ives</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Variations_on_%22America%22" title="Variations on &quot;America&quot;">Variations on "America"</a></i> for organ in 1891 at age seventeen. It included a polytonal section in three simultaneous keys, though this was omitted from performances at his father\'s request, because "it made the boys laugh out loud". Ives was fond of the rapid pedal line in the final variation, which he said was "almost as much fun as playing baseball". The piece was not published until 1949; the final version includes an introduction, seven variations and a polytonal interlude. The piece was adapted for orchestra in 1963 by <a href="/wiki/William_Schuman" title="William Schuman">William Schuman</a>. This version became popular during the bicentennial celebrations, and is often heard at pops concerts.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Muthuswami_Dikshitar" title="Muthuswami Dikshitar">Muthuswami Dikshitar</a> (1776\xe2\x80\x931835), one of the musical trinity in South Indian classical (<a href="/wiki/Carnatic_music" title="Carnatic music">Carnatic</a>) music composed some <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> pieces set to Western tunes. These are in the raga <a href="/wiki/Dheerasankarabharanam" class="mw-redirect" title="Dheerasankarabharanam">Sankarabharanam</a> and are referred to as "nottu swaras". Among these, the composition "Santatam Pahimam Sangita Shyamale" is set to the tune of "God Save the Queen".\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sigismond_Thalberg" title="Sigismond Thalberg">Sigismond Thalberg</a> (1812-1871), Swiss composer and one of the most famous virtuoso pianists of the 19th century, wrote a <a href="/wiki/Fantasia_(music)" title="Fantasia (music)">fantasia</a> on "God Save the Queen".\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Johan_Nepomuk_Hummel" class="mw-redirect" title="Johan Nepomuk Hummel">Johan Nepomuk Hummel</a> (1778-1837) wrote the <i>Variations from God Save the King in D major</i>, op. 10 and quoted the tune briefly in his <i>Freudenfest-Ouverture in D major</i> S 148\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Adrien-Fran%C3%A7ois_Servais" title="Adrien-Fran\xc3\xa7ois Servais">Adrien-Fran\xc3\xa7ois Servais</a> (1807\xe2\x80\x9366) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Ghys&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Joseph Ghys (page does not exist)">Joseph Ghys</a> (1801\xe2\x80\x9348) wrote <i>Variations brillantes et concertantes sur l\'air "God Save the King"</i>, op. 38, for violin and cello and performed it in London and St Petersburg.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Georges_Onslow" class="mw-redirect" title="Georges Onslow">Georges Onslow</a> (1784-1853) used the tune in his String Quartet No. 7 in G Minor, op.9, second movement.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Jacob" title="Gordon Jacob">Gordon Jacob</a> wrote a choral arrangement of God Save the Queen with a trumpet fanfare introduction, for the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II</a> in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rock_adaptations">Rock adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=God_Save_the_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Rock adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The British rock band <a href="/wiki/Family_(band)" title="Family (band)">Family</a> sampled "God Save the Queen" at the end of their 1968 debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Music_in_a_Doll%27s_House" title="Music in a Doll&#39;s House">Music in a Doll\'s House</a></i>, following the final track, "3 x Time".\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> performed an impromptu version of "God Save the Queen" during their 30 January 1969 rooftop concert, atop the <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">Apple</a> building.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> They had also whistled the melody of the song on their first fan club Christmas record in 1963.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Jimi_Hendrix_Experience" title="The Jimi Hendrix Experience">The Jimi Hendrix Experience</a> played an impromptu version of "God Save the Queen" to open his set at the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight_Festival_1970" title="Isle of Wight Festival 1970">Isle of Wight Festival 1970</a>. Just before walking onto the stage, he can be seen (on the DVD) and heard to ask "How does it go again?" in reference to said UK national anthem. He may have been able to simply hear it mimicked by voice and then perform it,<sup id="cite_ref-HOPK_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOPK-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> though the same melody was (and is) widely known in the United States to the lyrics "My country, \'tis of thee". Hendrix gave the same sort of distortion and improvisation of "God Save the Queen", as he had done with "<a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">The Star-Spangled Banner</a>" at the <a href="/wiki/Woodstock" title="Woodstock">Woodstock Festival</a>, 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-HOPK_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOPK-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gentle_Giant" title="Gentle Giant">Gentle Giant</a> performed a version\xe2\x80\x94titled <i>The Queen</i>\xe2\x80\x94on <a href="/wiki/Gentle_Giant_(album)" title="Gentle Giant (album)">their 1970 eponymous debut album</a>.\n</p><p>The rock band <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a> recorded an instrumental version of "God Save the Queen" on their 1975 album <i><a href="/wiki/A_Night_at_the_Opera_(Queen_album)" title="A Night at the Opera (Queen album)">A Night at the Opera</a></i>. It was arranged by guitarist <a href="/wiki/Brian_May" title="Brian May">Brian May</a> and features his distinctive layers of <a href="/wiki/Overdub" class="mw-redirect" title="Overdub">overdubbed</a> electric guitars. A tape of this version would be played at the end of almost every concert, with <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mercury" title="Freddie Mercury">Freddie Mercury</a> walking around the stage wearing a crown and a cloak on their <a href="/wiki/Magic_Tour_(Queen)" title="Magic Tour (Queen)">Magic Tour</a> in 1986. The song was played whilst all the Queen members would take their bows.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> On 3 June 2002, during the Queen\'s <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Jubilee">Golden Jubilee</a>, Brian May performed the anthem on his <a href="/wiki/Red_Special" title="Red Special">Red Special</a> electric guitar for <a href="/wiki/Party_at_the_Palace" title="Party at the Palace">Party at the Palace</a>, performing from the roof of <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a>, and features on the 30th Anniversary DVD edition of <i>A Night at the Opera</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> \n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vevent" (band)">Queen</a></th></tr><tr class="description"><th colspan="2" class="description" style="text-align:center;background-color:  #E6E8FA;">from the album <i><a
